  /** Converts a <button> to look like an <a> tag. */
  .linkify {
    display: inline;
    padding: 0;
    margin: 0;
    border: 0;
    background: 0;
    cursor: pointer;
  }
  h1, h2, h3, h4 {
    background: none;
  }
  a, chops-button, a.button, .button, .linkify {
    color: var(--chops-link-color);
    text-decoration: none;
    font-weight: var(--chops-link-font-weight);
    font-family: var(--chops-font-family);
  }
  a:hover, .linkify:hover {
    text-decoration: underline;
  }
  a.button, .button {
    /* Links that look like buttons. */
    display: inline-flex;
    align-items: center;
    justify-content: center;
    text-decoration: none;
    transition: filter 0.3s ease-in-out, box-shadow 0.3s ease-in-out;
  }
  a.button:hover, .button:hover {
    filter: brightness(95%);
  }
  chops-button, a.button, .button {
    box-sizing: border-box;
    font-size: var(--chops-main-font-size);
    background: var(--chops-white);
    border-radius: 6px;
    --chops-button-padding: 0.25em 8px;
    margin: 0;
    margin-left: auto;
  }
  a.button, .button {
    padding: var(--chops-button-padding);
  }
  chops-button i.material-icons, a.button i.material-icons, .button i.material-icons {
    display: block;
    margin-right: 4px;
  }
  chops-button.emphasized, a.button.emphasized, .button.emphasized {
    background: var(--chops-primary-button-bg);
    color: var(--chops-primary-button-color);
    text-shadow: 1px 1px 3px hsla(0, 0%, 0%, 0.25);
  }
  textarea, select, input {
    box-sizing: border-box;
    font-size: var(--chops-main-font-size);
  }
  /* Note: decoupling heading levels from styles is useful for
  * accessibility because styles will not always line up with semantically
  * appropriate heading levels.
  */
  .medium-heading {
    font-size: var(--chops-large-font-size);
    font-weight: normal;
    line-height: 1;
    padding: 0.25em 0;
    color: var(--chops-link-color);
    margin: 0;
    margin-top: 0.25em;
    border-bottom: var(--chops-normal-border);
  }
  .medium-heading chops-button {
    line-height: 1.6;
  }
  .input-grid {
    padding: 0.5em 0;
    display: grid;
    max-width: 100%;

    grid-template-columns: minmax(120px, max-content) 1fr;
    align-items: flex-start;
  }
  .input-grid label {
    font-weight: bold;
    text-align: right;
    word-wrap: break-word;
  }
  @media (max-width: 600px) {
    .input-grid label {

      text-align: left;
    }
    .input-grid {
      grid-gap: var(--mr-input-grid-gap);
      grid-template-columns: 100%;
    }
  }

          --mr-toggled-font-family: Monospace;
        }
        .container-issue {
          width: 100%;

        }

Email display settings

<h2>Email display settings</h2>



        

There is a 

setting

<a href="/hosting/settings">setting</a>

 to control how
        your email address appears on comments and issues that you post.

<p>There is a <a href="/hosting/settings">setting</a> to control how
        your email address appears on comments and issues that you post.</p>



        

Project members will always see your full email address.  By
        default, other users who visit the site will see an
        abbreviated version of your email address.

<p>Project members will always see your full email address.  By
        default, other users who visit the site will see an
        abbreviated version of your email address.</p>



        

If you do not wish your email address to be shared, there
        are other ways to 

get
        involved

<a href="http://www.chromium.org/getting-involved">get
        involved</a>

 in the community.  To report a problem when using
        the Chrome browser, you may use the "Report an issue..."  item
        on the "Help" menu.

<p>If you do not wish your email address to be shared, there
        are other ways to <a href="http://www.chromium.org/getting-involved">get
        involved</a> in the community.  To report a problem when using
        the Chrome browser, you may use the "Report an issue..."  item
        on the "Help" menu.</p>

This site hosts public issues in public projects

<h2>This site hosts public issues in public projects</h2>



        

Unlike our internal issue tracker, this site makes most
        issues public, unless the issue is labeled with a Restrict-View-*
        label, such as Restrict-View-Google.

<p>Unlike our internal issue tracker, this site makes most
        issues public, unless the issue is labeled with a Restrict-View-*
        label, such as Restrict-View-Google.</p>



        

Components are not used for permissions.  And, regardless of
        restriction labels, the issue reporter, owner,
        and Cc'd users may always view the issue.

<p>Components are not used for permissions.  And, regardless of
        restriction labels, the issue reporter, owner,
        and Cc'd users may always view the issue.</p>

Chrome Version: 107.0.5304.92 (Official Build) (64-bit) 



<span class="line"><!---->Chrome Version: 107.0.5304.92 (Official Build) (64-bit) <!----></span>



<br>



<br>



<br>



<br>





What steps will reproduce the problem?



<b class="line"><!---->What steps will reproduce the problem?<!----></b>



<br>





(1)



<b class="line"><!---->(1)<!----></b>





 Download the "WebStoreImitatorDemo.zip" attached to this report.



<span class="line"><!----> Download the "WebStoreImitatorDemo.zip" attached to this report.<!----></span>



<br>





(2)



<b class="line"><!---->(2)<!----></b>





 Turn on developer mode in chrome://extensions and drag the zip into it.



<span class="line"><!----> Turn on developer mode in chrome://extensions and drag the zip into it.<!----></span>



<br>



<br>



<br>



<br>





What is the expected result?



<b class="line"><!---->What is the expected result?<!----></b>



<br>



<br>





The extension tries to use chrome.webstorePrivate. Since only the component Web Store extension (ahfgeienlihckogmohjhadlkjgocpleb) can do that, it should not work. The "NOKEY" version of this file will throw an error as normal.



<span class="line"><!---->The extension tries to use chrome.webstorePrivate. Since only the component Web Store extension (ahfgeienlihckogmohjhadlkjgocpleb) can do that, it should not work. The "NOKEY" version of this file will throw an error as normal.<!----></span>



<br>



<br>



<br>



<br>





What happens instead?



<b class="line"><!---->What happens instead?<!----></b>



<br>



<br>





Since the extension uses the Web Store's public key [1], it can fix its ID to match and override the Web Store extension. This puts it on the whitelist for extensions that can use chrome.webstorePrivate, so it can prompt you to install extensions/apps.



<span class="line"><!---->Since the extension uses the Web Store's public key [1], it can fix its ID to match and override the Web Store extension. This puts it on the whitelist for extensions that can use chrome.webstorePrivate, so it can prompt you to install extensions/apps.<!----></span>



<br>



<br>



<br>



<br>





Please provide any additional information below. Attach a screenshot if



<b class="line"><!---->Please provide any additional information below. Attach a screenshot if<!----></b>



<br>





possible.



<b class="line"><!---->possible.<!----></b>



<br>



<br>





If you can find the public key of a trusted Chrome extension ID (not that hard), you can put it in the key value in the manifest of your crafted Chrome extension. Your Chrome extension will now have a fixed ID and will be on the whitelist for using special and possibly unsafe APIs that an extension normally could not access.



<span class="line"><!---->If you can find the public key of a trusted Chrome extension ID (not that hard), you can put it in the key value in the manifest of your crafted Chrome extension. Your Chrome extension will now have a fixed ID and will be on the whitelist for using special and possibly unsafe APIs that an extension normally could not access.<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





It's unlikely that extensions hosted on the Chrome Web Store can fix their public key to a value (and they certainly can’t override another extension). For this reason, I doubt that it's possible to attack somebody by installing an extension from the Chrome Web Store.



<span class="line"><!---->It's unlikely that extensions hosted on the Chrome Web Store can fix their public key to a value (and they certainly can’t override another extension). For this reason, I doubt that it's possible to attack somebody by installing an extension from the Chrome Web Store.<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





But if an attacker can convince someone to install their extension from chrome://extensions, and the extension has a spoofed public key, the extension can basically access any private Chrome API.



<span class="line"><!---->But if an attacker can convince someone to install their extension from chrome://extensions, and the extension has a spoofed public key, the extension can basically access any private Chrome API.<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





webstorePrivate is not the only dangerous private API. autofillPrivate, chromeosInfoPrivate, terminalPrivate, settingsPrivate, and others [2] could easily be used for malicious purposes.



<span class="line"><!---->webstorePrivate is not the only dangerous private API. autofillPrivate, chromeosInfoPrivate, terminalPrivate, settingsPrivate, and others [2] could easily be used for malicious purposes.<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





On ChromeOS, a malicious extension could use terminalPrivate to run important commands in crosh, such as rollback or live_in_a_coal_mine. Some extensions could also run on the ChromeOS sign-in screen [3]. Of course, the fact that settingsPrivate is accessible is also very concerning.



<span class="line"><!---->On ChromeOS, a malicious extension could use terminalPrivate to run important commands in crosh, such as rollback or live_in_a_coal_mine. Some extensions could also run on the ChromeOS sign-in screen [3]. Of course, the fact that settingsPrivate is accessible is also very concerning.<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





TL;DR: Using a public key to fix an extension ID in manifest.json basically ruins the whole point of "private" Chrome APIs.



<span class="line"><!---->TL;DR: Using a public key to fix an extension ID in manifest.json basically ruins the whole point of "private" Chrome APIs.<!----></span>



<br>



<br>



<br>



<br>

Here are a few other interesting POCs utilizing this bug:



<span class="line"><!---->Here are a few other interesting POCs utilizing this bug:<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





File 1: Can infinitely quit the browser (ChromeOS only, don't worry, it won't quit the browser without you pressing a button each time)



<span class="line"><!---->File 1: Can infinitely quit the browser (ChromeOS only, don't worry, it won't quit the browser without you pressing a button each time)<!----></span>



<br>





File 2: A textbox keylogger that runs on all applications and websites (ChromeOS only)



<span class="line"><!---->File 2: A textbox keylogger that runs on all applications and websites (ChromeOS only)<!----></span>



<br>





File 3: An extension with chrome.processes perms (haven't looked into this too much but it looks dangerous, works on all platforms)



<span class="line"><!---->File 3: An extension with chrome.processes perms (haven't looked into this too much but it looks dangerous, works on all platforms)<!----></span>



<br>





File 4: An extension with chrome.terminalPrivate perms (you can do all the dangerous powerwashing stuff with rollback, ChromeOS only)



<span class="line"><!---->File 4: An extension with chrome.terminalPrivate perms (you can do all the dangerous powerwashing stuff with rollback, ChromeOS only)<!----></span>

I'm not sure this threat is within scope here since if an extension can be loaded locally and never be placed on the extension store then I think this counts as just local access, in which case an attacker can do anything they want to the running Chrome.



<span class="line"><!---->I'm not sure this threat is within scope here since if an extension can be loaded locally and never be placed on the extension store then I think this counts as just local access, in which case an attacker can do anything they want to the running Chrome.<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





I'm not sure if it's WAI that extension ids can be spoofed by locally installed (non-webstore) extensions, it sounds like it might be, in which case this is most certainly not a security bug and might even be a WontFix



<span class="line"><!---->I'm not sure if it's WAI that extension ids can be spoofed by locally installed (non-webstore) extensions, it sounds like it might be, in which case this is most certainly not a security bug and might even be a WontFix<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





I'll let the extensions team decide.



<span class="line"><!---->I'll let the extensions team decide.<!----></span>

Okay, just wanted to mention that there are a couple more very dangerous APIs that can be accessed with this bug on all platforms.



<span class="line"><!---->Okay, just wanted to mention that there are a couple more very dangerous APIs that can be accessed with this bug on all platforms.<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





File 1: An extension with chrome.settingsPrivate perms (you can edit any Chrome browser setting)



<span class="line"><!---->File 1: An extension with chrome.settingsPrivate perms (you can edit any Chrome browser setting)<!----></span>



<br>





File 2: An packaged app with chrome.developerPrivate perms (you can inspect other extensions, install them without prompts, etc)



<span class="line"><!---->File 2: An packaged app with chrome.developerPrivate perms (you can inspect other extensions, install them without prompts, etc)<!----></span>

I don't really know if installing an extension from chrome://extensions is nessescarily local access. You could pretend to send somebody a verified CRX or something like that, and ask them to install it. I think the overall expectation when installing a CRX is that it shouldn't be able to change your settings, powerwash your device, keylog you everywhere, etc, and this bug ruins the safety of installing an extension.



<span class="line"><!---->I don't really know if installing an extension from chrome://extensions is nessescarily local access. You could pretend to send somebody a verified CRX or something like that, and ask them to install it. I think the overall expectation when installing a CRX is that it shouldn't be able to change your settings, powerwash your device, keylog you everywhere, etc, and this bug ruins the safety of installing an extension.<!----></span>

Possible impact of pushing via the admin console though might be something along the lines of a privilege escalation on a users personal device. This can be caused by the fact that even on an un-managed device if a work account is signed in the forced extension will automatically install.  I have written up a good scenario of a situation where this could be abused:



<span class="line"><!---->Possible impact of pushing via the admin console though might be something along the lines of a privilege escalation on a users personal device. This can be caused by the fact that even on an un-managed device if a work account is signed in the forced extension will automatically install.  I have written up a good scenario of a situation where this could be abused:<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





Bob is a Google Workspace sysadmin.



<span class="line"><!---->Bob is a Google Workspace sysadmin.<!----></span>



<br>





He creates the malicious extension and adds it to the force install list of his organization. 



<span class="line"><!---->He creates the malicious extension and adds it to the force install list of his organization. <!----></span>



<br>





Bob goes to Alice and says hey, I've created this new google account for you, would you mind signing in and activating it.



<span class="line"><!---->Bob goes to Alice and says hey, I've created this new google account for you, would you mind signing in and activating it.<!----></span>



<br>





People generally inherently trust a google account, I mean why wouldn't you.  The domain is valid, its not the phishing attack you may have been trained for.



<span class="line"><!---->People generally inherently trust a google account, I mean why wouldn't you.  The domain is valid, its not the phishing attack you may have been trained for.<!----></span>



<br>





Alice goes to the Google Workspace invite email she has been sent(By Google) and activates the account (either on her work or home computer)



<span class="line"><!---->Alice goes to the Google Workspace invite email she has been sent(By Google) and activates the account (either on her work or home computer)<!----></span>



<br>





Upon activation the account creates a new browser profile and install all extensions enforced by the Google Admin Console, which at this point includes the malicious extension.



<span class="line"><!---->Upon activation the account creates a new browser profile and install all extensions enforced by the Google Admin Console, which at this point includes the malicious extension.<!----></span>



<br>





Upon install Bob now has escalated control over Alice's work or home device via the malicious extension.



<span class="line"><!---->Upon install Bob now has escalated control over Alice's work or home device via the malicious extension.<!----></span>

This is not a security bug.  Unpacked extensions can override their ID using the "key" field in the manifest.json file; this is publicly documented [1], and allows extensions to use private APIs.  This is outside our threat model, and is already entered in our public extensions security FAQ [2].  When you create a .crx file, it will override the "key" entry in the manifest with the public key of the PEM file used, so it's not possible to upload or distribute a verified CRX without a private key for the associated ID.



<span class="line"><!---->This is not a security bug.  Unpacked extensions can override their ID using the "key" field in the manifest.json file; this is publicly documented [1], and allows extensions to use private APIs.  This is outside our threat model, and is already entered in our public extensions security FAQ [2].  When you create a .crx file, it will override the "key" entry in the manifest with the public key of the PEM file used, so it's not possible to upload or distribute a verified CRX without a private key for the associated ID.<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





[1] 



<span class="line"><!---->[1] <!----></span>





https://developer.chrome.com/docs/extensions/mv3/manifest/key/



<a class="undefined" target="_blank" href="https://developer.chrome.com/docs/extensions/mv3/manifest/key/"><!---->https://developer.chrome.com/docs/extensions/mv3/manifest/key/<!----></a>



<br>





[2] 



<span class="line"><!---->[2] <!----></span>





https://chromium.googlesource.com/chromium/src/+/main/extensions/docs/security_faq.md#Is-accessing-private-APIs-via-unpacked-extensions-a-security-bug
